Precedent	Such is the de facto doctrine, born of necessity and public policy to prevent needless confusion and endless mischief."
Precedent	This Court also further held that the validity of the appointment cannot be challenged in collateral proceedings.
Precedent	It is true that in the light of the finding that Section 9(7) is invalid, violative of the basic structure and judicial independence envisaged in the Constitution, public policy requires that the doctrine of de facto be engrafted on necessity to protect the interest of the public and the individuals involved in the official acts of persons exercising the duty of an office without actually being one in strict point of law.
Precedent	Therefore, though, de jure they are not by title validly appointed, but by color o f title the exercise and functions as Judge of the Designated Court, trials conducted, judgments rendered, orders passed, punishments imposed and convictions made are legal and valid.
Precedent	The de facto doctrine is not a stranger to the Constitution or to the <ORG> and the legislature of the States.
Precedent	Article 233-A recognises this doctrine brought by Constitution Twentieth Amendment Act, 1966.
Precedent	Therefore, the trials conducted, judgments pronounced and the orders or punishment imposed under the Act remained valid.
Precedent	423.
Precedent	Thus it must be held that the confessions recorded by any police officer below the rank of Superintendent of Police under Section 15(1) and the appointment of Sessions and Additional Sessions Judges to the Designated Court under Section 9(7) are unconstitutional.
Precedent	Yet the confessions so recorded by exercising the power under Section 15(1) shall remain valid and would be considered at the trial or in appeal in accordance with law.
Precedent	Any judgment or order made and conviction rendered exercising powers u nder the Act and sentence imposed relying thereon does not become invalid or void.
Precedent	We further hold that it is open to the <ORG> to amend Sections 9(7) and 15(1) of the Act suitably..
Precedent	The operation of this judgment is postponed for a year from today to carry out the amendments and necessary steps be taken to have Sections 15(1) and 9(7) suitably amended.
Precedent	If no amendments are effected within the period or extended period on and from the date of expiry of the period aforementioned, or any extended time by order of this Court, Section 15(1) and Section 9(7) would thereafter become void.
Precedent	146 : 1981 SCC (Cri) 652: (1981) 3 SCR 474   424.
Precedent	The further question is whether <ORG> would be justified to exercise its power under Article 226 of the Constitution in respect of the matters covered under the Act?
Precedent	425.
Precedent	The legislature treated terrorism as a special criminal problem under the Act and the ordinary criminal courts created under the Code were divested of the power and jurisdiction to try the offenses  governed under the Act and invested the same in <ORG> and appellate powers to this Court.
Precedent	426.
RatioOfTheDecision	From the scheme of the Act therefore it is clear that the offenses created thereunder are exclusively triable by <ORG> and conviction made or orders passed, whether final or interlocutory orders pending trial are regulated under the provisions of the Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	Right of appeal thereon has been provided by Section 19 to this Court.
RatioOfTheDecision	Under the Code <ORG> and <ORG> play major role in the administration of criminal justice, from the stage of arrest of an accused or suspect till the trial is concluded or conviction became final.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> has jurisdiction and control over <ORG> or the Magistrate, but under the scheme of the Act there is a wall of separation and complete exclusion of the jurisdiction of <ORG> is total.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> is neither subordinate to <ORG>, nor <ORG> has any control or supervisory jurisdiction und er Article 227.
Precedent	427.
Precedent	From this scenario, the question emerges whether <ORG> under Article 226 would be right in entertaining proceedings to quash the charge-sheet or to grant bail to a person accused of an offence under the Act or other offenses committed during the course of the same transaction exclusively triable by <ORG>.
Precedent	Nothing is more conspicuous than the failure of law to evolve a consistent jurisdictional doctrine or even elementary principles, if it is subject to conflicting or inconceivable or inconsistent result which lead to uncertainty, incongruity and disbelief in the efficacy of law.
Precedent	The jurisdiction and power of <ORG> under Article 226 of the Constitution is undoubtedly constituent power and <ORG> has untrammeled powers and jurisdiction to issue any writ or order or direction to any person or authority within its territorial jurisdiction for enforcement of any of the fundamental rights or for any other purpose.
Precedent	The legislature has no power to divest  the court of the constituent power engrafted under Article 226.
Precedent	A superior court is deemed to have general jurisdiction and the law presumes that the court has acted within its jurisdiction.
Precedent	This presumption is denied to the inferior courts.
Precedent	The judgment of a superior court unreservedly is conclusive as to all relevant matters thereby decided, while the judgment of the inferior court involving a question of jurisdiction is not final.
Precedent	The superior court, therefore, has jurisdiction to determine its own jurisdiction, may be rightly or wrongly.
Precedent	Therefore, the court in an appropriate proceeding may erroneously exercise jurisdiction.
Precedent	It does not constitute want of jurisdiction, but it impinges upon its propriety in the exercise of the jurisdiction.
Precedent	Want of jurisdiction can be established solely by a superior court and that in practice no decision can be impeached collaterally by an inferior court.
Precedent	However, acts done by a superior court are always deemed valid wherever they are relied upon.
RatioOfTheDecision	The exclusion thereof f rom the rule of validity is indispensable in its finality.
RatioOfTheDecision	The superior courts, therefore, are the final arbiters of the validity of the acts done not only by other inferior courts or authorities, but also their own decisions.
RatioOfTheDecision	Though they are immune from collateral attack, but to avoid confusion the superior court's decisions lay down the rules of validity; are not governed by those rules.
RatioOfTheDecision	The valid decision is not only conclusive, it may affect, but it is .also conclusive in proceedings where it is sought to be collaterally impeached.
RatioOfTheDecision	However, the term conclusiveness may acquire other specific meanings.
Precedent	It may mean that the finding upon which the decision is founded as distinct or it is the operative part or has to be conclusive or these findings bind only parties on litigated disputes or that the organ which has made the decision is itself precluded from revoking, rescinding or otherwise altering it.
Precedent	428.
Precedent	The decision or order or a writ issued by <ORG> under Article 226 is subject to judicial revi ew by an appeal to this Court under Article 136 whose sweep is wide and untrammeled.
Precedent	The question, therefore, is whether <ORG> would be proper to exercise its power under Article 226 over the proceedings or the offenses, or the other offenses committed in the course of the same transaction, covered under the Act.
Precedent	The jurisdiction of <ORG> though was not expressly excluded under the Act, by necessary implication it gets eclipsed not so much that it lacked constituent power but by doctrine of concomitance.
Precedent	429.
Precedent	In <ORG>, Re; <NAME> v. <ORG> 147 the facts were that <ORG> and <ORG> have coordinate jurisdiction over debenture holder of a company carrying on business in the County Palatine of Lancaster.
Precedent	When the debenture holder was indicted of an offence of cheating, <ORG> and the <ORG> simultaneously had taken cognizance of the offence on a motion, <ORG> issued an injunction restraining the plaintiff in the Palat ine action while the proceedings in <ORG> had jurisdiction to grant the same injunction.
Precedent	The question was whether the <ORG> was justified in taking cognizance and issuance of the injunction prayed for.
Precedent	<NAME>, J. as he then was, exercising the jurisdiction of <ORG> issued the injunction restraining the plaintiff in the <ORG> from proceeding with the action.
Precedent	On appeal, <NAME>, L.J., of <ORG>, as he then was, held that a man has a right to bring an action in a court of inferior jurisdiction when the circumstances of the case entitle him to do so and if he is within the right, he is neither more nor less liable to be restrained from proceeding with an action in a court of coordinate jurisdiction.
Precedent	The question of jurisdiction to grant the injunction has nothing to do with the status of the court.
Precedent	It has to do with the circumstances of the case as bearing on the 147 (1911)ICh731:80LJCh409 conduct of the party enjoined.
Precedent	That being so, the court held tha t the case turns upon propriety of making the order.
Precedent	The existence of the jurisdiction does not warrant the court in exercising it on occasions when its exercise is not fully justified by the facts of the case.
Precedent	It held that since <ORG> has avoided vexation, <ORG> was justified in exercising the jurisdiction in issuing the injunction.
Precedent	430.
Precedent	In <ORG> v. Attorney General148 the plaintiff <ORG> company launched sales promotion known as "spot cash" for a particular brand of cigarette.
Argument	The <ORG> laid prosecution in <ORG> against the company under Lotteries and Amusement Act, 1976.
Argument	The plaintiff initiated action in <ORG>, <ORG>, seeking a declaration that their schemes are lawful.
Argument	Before charges were tried in <ORG>, <ORG> took a preliminary objection that <ORG> has no jurisdiction to grant declaration sought for and if it were to be held in its jurisdiction it regulated to declin e to be summoned the declaration on the ground that criminal case was already pending in the coordinate jurisdiction, namely <ORG>.
Argument	In that context <ORG> through <ORG> following <ORG> case147 held that the case is one of concurrent jurisdiction, <ORG> being a court of coordinate jurisdiction, it is unusual that the court of coordinate jurisdiction is a criminal court.
Argument	This is clearly a major factor to be taken into account is deciding whether <ORG> in its discretion, to assume or decline jurisdiction to hear the summons on its merits.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was held:  " The basic principles are not in doubt.
RatioOfTheDecision	The object of all procedural rules is to enable justice to be done between the parties consistently with the public interest.
RatioOfTheDecision	So, the choice between courts of concurrent jurisdiction must always depend on where and how justice can best be done.
RatioOfTheDecision	Many factors have to be considered, but, where the conflict lies between courts of civil and criminal jurisdiction,  the most important consideration is the obvious one: criminal courts exist to deal with criminal matters, and their procedural rules are designed for that purpose.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is only in those relatively rare cases where the sole issue is one of law that a case can be made for <ORG> to assume jurisdiction.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is because, there being no issue of fact to be determined., trial by jury is otiose; the issue of guilt will be determined by the judge of <ORG> on submission of law, leading inevitably to a formal direction to the jury to acquit or convict, as the case may be.
RatioOfTheDecision	The criminal procedure, is no better designed, indeed it is often less well adapted than the civil procedure to determine pure questions of law.
RatioOfTheDecision	Appeals in either case lie to the same court.
Precedent	Where issues of law can best be determined, therefore, is essentially a question of convenience in the true sense of that word."
Precedent	148 (1979) 2 All ER 592: (1979) 2 WLR 805 147 (1911) 1 Ch 731: 80 LJ Ch 409 Accordingly it was held that when the proc eedings were initiated in <ORG>, though <ORG> was the court of coordinate jurisdiction, the matter being pure question of law untrammeled by questions of facts, the summons issued by <ORG> were held to be efficacious and upheld.
Precedent	431.
Argument	<CITATION_1326302> 149 the Commissioner and the tribunal under Bombay Sales Tax Act had concurrent jurisdiction to entertain revision against the orders of the Dy.
Argument	Commissioner.
Argument	When the proceedings before the tribunal were pending, the Commissioner entertained the revisional jurisdiction.
Argument	When the propriety of the exercise thereunder was questioned, <ORG> upheld the exercise of the jurisdiction by the Commissioner on the ground that the tribunal cannot decide the matter on merits.
Precedent	On appeal this <ORG> held thus: (SCC p. 470, para  8) "Now it seems to us past question that when the appellate jurisdiction of superior authority is invoked against an order and that authority is seized of the case, it is inconceivable for a subordinate authority  to claim to exercise jurisdiction to revise that very order.
Precedent	The tribunal is the supreme appellate and revisional authority under the statute.
Precedent	It cannot be divested of its jurisdiction to decide on the correctness of an order, it cannot be frustrated in the exercise of that jurisdiction, merely because a subordinate authority, the Commissioner, has also been vested with jurisdiction over that order.
Precedent	Unless the statute plainly provides to the contrary, that appears to us to be incontrovertible.
Precedent	It is not open to the Commissioner to invoke his power under clause  (a) of sub-section (1) of Section 57 and summon the record of an order over which the tribunal has already assumed appellate jurisdiction.
Precedent	The subordinate status of the Commissioner precludes that."

Precedent	434.
Precedent	In <NAME> v. <NAME> <ORG> of the United States of America in considering the question of issuing habeas corpus, the prisoner whether would come within the State Act or the federal constitution, it was held that <ORG> must observe the doctrine of comity and stated thus:  "[T]he doctrine of comity  teaches that one court should defer action on causes properly within its jurisdiction until the court of another sovereignty with concurrent powers, and already cognizant of the litigation, has had an opportunity to pass upon the matter."
Precedent	This principle was reiterated in <NAME> v. <NAME>.
Precedent	In <NAME> v. <ORG> 154 it was held that federal courts confronted with requests to interfere with state civil functions should abide by standards of restraint that go well beyond those of private equity jurisprudence.
Precedent	In United States v. <NAME> it was held that while principles of comity command careful consideration by federal courts, comity must yield where important federal interests are at stake, such as in the enforcement of federal criminal statutes.
RatioOfTheDecision	435.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus it could be seen that though <ORG> has jurisdiction and power under Article 226 to issue appropriate writ or direction or order in exceptional cases at the behest of a person accused of an offence tria ble under the Act or offence jointly triable with the offenses under the Act, <ORG> being amenable to appellate jurisdiction and judicial review under Article 136 to this <ORG>, and this <ORG> having been statutorily invested with the power and jurisdiction under Section 19 of the Act, judicial pragmatism, concomitance between this <ORG> and <ORG>, the latter must observe comity and self-imposed limitation on the exercise of the power under Article 226 and refuse to pass an order or to give direction which would inevitably result in exercising the jurisdiction and power 117  152 339 US 200: 94 L Ed 761 (1949) 153 401 US 37: 27 L Ed 2d 669 (1971) 154 420 US 592: 43 L Ed 2d 482 (1975) 155 445 US 360: 63 L Ed 2d 454 (1980) conferred on this <ORG> under Section 19 of the Act or sitting over the appellate orders passed by this <ORG>.

RatioOfTheDecision	Exercise of the power even in exceptional cases or circumstances is, therefore, incompatible with or inconsistent with comity.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, the only check up on a court's exercise of power is ones own sense of self-restraint and due respect to comity.
RatioOfTheDecision	Judicial pragmatism, therefore, poignantly point, per force to observe constitutional propriety and comity imposing self- discipline to decline to entertain proceedings under Article 226 over the matters covered under Section 19 or the matters in respect of which remedy under Section 19 is available or taken cognizance; issue of process or prima facie case in the complaint or charge-sheet etc., in other words all matters covered under the Act.

Precedent	438.
Precedent	Various provisions of the Terrorists and Disruptive Activities (Prevention) Act, 1987 (Act 28 of 1987) and Act 31 of 1985 (hereinafter referred to as 'TADA') "enacted to make special provision for the prevention of, and for coping with, terrorists and disruptive activities and for matte rs connected therewith or incidental thereto", were assailed not only for infraction of fundamental rights guaranteed by the Constitution but also for being in violation of fair trial, the sine qua non of any civilised criminal jurisprudence.

RatioOfTheDecision	752  439.
RatioOfTheDecision	Terror according to  dictionary means, 'extreme fear or fright'.
RatioOfTheDecision	But 'terrorist' and 'terrorism' have become associated with "ideology of overthrowing a government by resorting to violent fear inspiring methods", "opposition to government by methods which excite fear or any series of terrifying, unlawful deeds which tend to intimidate".
RatioOfTheDecision	Some "consider it as a desperate response of the growing number of weak or powerless groups challenging the rigidities of frontiers, powers and resource of distribution".
RatioOfTheDecision	An abused understanding of the terrorism is said to be "pejorative for freedom fighting or rebellion disapproved by the authorities".
RatioOfTheDecision	Terrorism politically is "coercive intimidation".
RatioOfTheDecision	Systematic use of murder and destruction to instill the feeling of fear and terror in one or all, individual or group, institutions or government is its acknowledged method.
RatioOfTheDecision	The most reprehensible part of it is that its victims are usually innocent persons having nothing to do either with politics or government.
RatioOfTheDecision	Whatever their ideology or coloring,  terrorists are desperate people bitterly opposed to the prevailing regime.
RatioOfTheDecision	"They are fond of using romantic euphemism for their murderous crime.
RatioOfTheDecision	They claim to be revolutionary heroes yet they commit cowardly acts and lack the heroic qualities of humanity and magnanimity.
RatioOfTheDecision	They profess to be revolutionaries yet they attack only by stealth, murder and maim the innocent.
Precedent	They claim to bring liberation whereas in reality they seek power for themselves."
Precedent	[Terrorism and the Liberal State by <NAME>].
Precedent	440.
RatioOfTheDecision	Terrorism is a global phenomenon.
RatioOfTheDecision	Hijacking, diplomatic killings, bombing, kidnapping, innocent murders, destruction have become order of the day.
RatioOfTheDecision	It may be politically motivated or revolutionary in outlook or sponsored by one country against other in shape of proxy war.
RatioOfTheDecision	But in either case its method being violative of human rights it is neither legally justified nor ethically acceptable.
RatioOfTheDecision	In our country terrorism unlike European countries such as <NAME> of gang of West Germany or the Japanese <ORG> my, or Italy's <ORG>, or <ORG> in Ireland, is described as the, "classical manifestation of, sponsored terrorism".
RatioOfTheDecision	The objective of such unconventional war is to destabilise and weaken the Government and break up the social, political and economic order.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is adopted by one country against another by promoting use of violence and encouraging disruptive activities, feeding vague imaginations of the misguided, extending false hopes and promises, providing financial assistance, weapons, training and sanctuary.
RatioOfTheDecision	441.
RatioOfTheDecision	Terrorism, irrespective of its slogans, personal glorification, is an evil which cannot be tolerated by any society.
RatioOfTheDecision	No <ORG> can put up with it as it is responsible to protect its citizens, their lives, property, institutions and their legitimate and democratically elected Government.
RatioOfTheDecision	Protest by minority is the essence of democracy.
RatioOfTheDecision	Strike, boycott, marches, demonstration are legitimate methods of expressing dissatisfaction and inviting attention of' Government to the demands.
RatioOfTheDecision	The extreme form  of such political and moral pressure may be civil disobedience.
RatioOfTheDecision	But once the protest degenerates into violence it is opposed to basic democratic values.
RatioOfTheDecision	It shakes the rule of law the structural basis of any democracy.
RatioOfTheDecision	Whether such action is result of frustration or generated due to feeling of injustice or oppression it cannot be accepted as legitimate and legal by any civilised society, or any form of Government.
RatioOfTheDecision	It may be that founders of many nations were in a state of rebellion against existing order and were hailed as patriots on achieving their mission but that does not legitimise the methods adopted by terrorists or any political group as it largely depends on innocent killing and attacking soft targets.

Precedent	443.
RatioOfTheDecision	Having prefaced the discussion it may now be examined if the three enactments can be declared as invalid for being, 'legislative tyranny' or '<ORG> violence' of the fundamental rights guaranteed in Chapter III of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	But before entering upon an examination of different provisions of the Act it appears appropriate to deal, at the threshold, with the argument of legislative competence.
RatioOfTheDecision	In substance the submissions were twofold, one, that the subject-matter of the impugned legislation in pith and substance was public  order, which fell in exclusive domain of <ORG> under Entry 1 of List II, therefore, the power could not have been exercised by the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	And even if by straining the language of Entry 1 in List III it could be held to be criminal law the latter part of the entry operated as a bar on exercise of such power by the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Are these submissions well-founded?
RatioOfTheDecision	Power to frame or enact law for the governance of the country by the supreme body exercising the sovereign power is known as legislative power.
RatioOfTheDecision	In a democracy which has opted for federal structure of governance with a written Constitution the legislative powers either of the <ORG> or the <ORG> are derived from the Constitution itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	In our Constitution the <ORG> under Article 246 have plenary powers.
RatioOfTheDecision	Both are supreme in their sphere.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the field of legislative activity of the two sovereign <ORG> is regulated and is exercised in consonance with entries in List I and List II of the Seventh Schedule.
RatioOfTheDecision	Apart f rom exclusive field of activity provision is made empowering both the <ORG> to exercise legislative power in respect of any of the matters enumerated in List III in the Seventh Schedule known as Concurrent List.
RatioOfTheDecision	How these entries should be construed, what is the effect of their overlapping marginally have been discussed and explained by this <ORG> time and again.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, it is not necessary to recount all that.
Precedent	Although the learned Additional Solicitor General attempted to urge that the exercise of power could be traced to Entries 1, 2 and 2-A of List I and it has been accepted by Brother <NAME>, J., but I would confine it to the alternative submission made by the learned counsel that the legislation could be upheld under Entry 1 of List III which is extracted below :  "Criminal law, including all matters included in the Indian Penal Code at the commencement of this Constitution but excluding offenses against law with respect to any of the matters specified in List I or List II and excluding the us e of naval, military or air forces or any other armed forces of the Union in aid of the civil power."
Precedent	444.
Precedent	<CITATION_77461> it was observed: (SCC p. 174, para 8: AIR at p. 145 8) "The power to legislate is given to the appropriate legislatures by Article 246 of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The entries in the three Lists are only legislative heads or fields of legislation; they demarcate the area over which the appropriate legislatures can operate.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is well established that the widest amplitude should be given to the language of the entries.
RatioOfTheDecision	But some of the entries in the different lists or in the same list may overlap or may appear to be in direct conflict with each other.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is then the duty of this <ORG> to reconcile the entries and bring about a harmonious construction."
Precedent	445.
RatioOfTheDecision	From the language used it is apparent that the entry is couched in very wide terms.
RatioOfTheDecision	The words following the expression 'criminal law' enlarge the scope to any matter which can validly be considered to be criminal in nature.
RatioOfTheDecision	The exercise of p ower under thi s entry, therefore, has to be construed liberally so as to give full play to the legislative activity.
RatioOfTheDecision	The width of the entry, however, is controlled by the latter expression which takes away the power of either legislature to legislate in respect of offenses against laws with respect to any of the matters specified in List I or List II.
RatioOfTheDecision	Since this part 156 (1969) 2 SCC 166: AIR 1970 SC 1453 restricts and narrows the ambit of the entry it has to be construed strictly.
RatioOfTheDecision	Since under the federal structure the law made by the <ORG> has supremacy ( <CITATION_1235907> ) any enactment made in exercise of power under entry in Concurrent List shall have overriding effect subject to restrictions that may be spelt out from the entry itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	A legislation by Union <ORG> to be valid under this entry must satisfy two requirements; one, that it must relate to criminal law and the offence should not be such as has been or could be provided against laws with respect to any of the matters specified in L ist II.
RatioOfTheDecision	What is a criminal law?
RatioOfTheDecision	Any Act or rule dealing with crime, "(The) criminal justice system is a firmly societal defensive reaction to intolerable behavior.
RatioOfTheDecision	From the beginning it was considered as a tool designed to protect an established order of values attuned to the political organisation of the community.
RatioOfTheDecision	Transgression of some important norms reflecting these values was seen as a crime and, as such, demanded punishment."
RatioOfTheDecision	446.
RatioOfTheDecision	What is a crime in a given society at a particular time has a wide connotation as the concept of crime keeps on changing with change in political, economic and social set-up of the country.
RatioOfTheDecision	Various legislations dealing with economic offenses or offenses dealing with violation of industrial activity or breach of taxing provision are ample proof of it.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution-makers foresaw the eventuality, therefore they conferred such powers both on Central and State <ORG> to make laws in this regard.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such right includes power to define a crime and provide for its punishment .
RatioOfTheDecision	Use of the expression, "including all matters included in the Indian Penal Code at the commencement of the Constitution" is unequivocal indication of comprehensive nature of this entry.
RatioOfTheDecision	It further empowers the legislature to make laws not only in respect of matters covered by the Indian Penal Code but any other matter which could reasonably and justifiably be considered to be criminal in nature.
RatioOfTheDecision	Terrorist or disruptive activity is criminal in content, reach and effect.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> both, therefore, are empowered to legislate in respect of such an activity in exercise of the power conferred under Entry I of the Concurrent List.
RatioOfTheDecision	But this wide power is otherwise controlled and restricted by the latter part of the entry.
RatioOfTheDecision	It carves out an exception by precluding either of the legislatures from exercising the power if it is in respect of "offence against laws with respect to any of the matters specified in List I or II".
RatioOfTheDecision	The controversy, narrows down to whether the offenses under the TADA are  such in respect of which <ORG> could make a law.
RatioOfTheDecision	In other words if the legislation relating to TADA can fall in Entry 1 of List II then <ORG> would have competence to make a law under this entry and create offenses for violation of such law under Item 64 of List II and <ORG> would be precluded from making any law.
RatioOfTheDecision	But that would happen if it is held that law relating to TADA is either in fact or in pith and substance a law relating to, 157 : AIR 1972 SC 1061 'public order'.
RatioOfTheDecision	This expression was construed in <CITATION_456839> .
RatioOfTheDecision	It was held :  "Now 'public order' is an expression of wide connotation and signifies that state of tranquility prevailing among the members of a political society as a result of the internal regulations enforced by the Government which they have instituted."
RatioOfTheDecision	<CITATION_1733535> ' 9 it was observed as under:  "It will thus appear that just as 'public order' in the rulings of this <ORG> (earlier cited) was said to comprehend disorders of  less gravity than those affecting 'security of <ORG>', 'law and order' also comprehends disorders of less gravity than those affecting 'public order'.
RatioOfTheDecision	One has to imagine three concentric circles.
RatioOfTheDecision	Law and order represents the largest circle within which is the next circle representing public order and the smallest circle represents security of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is then easy to see that an act may affect law and order but not public order just as an act may affect public order but not security of the <ORG>."
RatioOfTheDecision	Can it be said that offenses dealt under TADA relate to public order?
RatioOfTheDecision	Is the distinction between public order as visualised in Entry 1 List II and TADA of degree only or are they substantially different?
RatioOfTheDecision	"Terrorism constitutes a direct repudiation of liberal and human values and principles, and that terrorist ideology is ... and constantly deployed in a struggle to defame and discredit democracy."
RatioOfTheDecision	The terrorism with which our country is faced has been described as explained earlier is sponsored terrorism.
RatioOfTheDecision	Terroris m whether it is sponsored or revolutionary or even political by its nature cannot be considered to be public order as explained by this Court.
RatioOfTheDecision	Conceptually public order and terrorism are different not only in ideology and philosophy but also in cause or the mens rea, the manner of its commission and the effect or result of such activity.
RatioOfTheDecision	Public order is well understood and fully comprehended as a problem associated with law and order.
RatioOfTheDecision	Terrorism is a new crime far serious in nature, more graver in impact, and highly dangerous in consequence.
RatioOfTheDecision	One pertains to law and order problem whereas the other may be political in nature coupled with unjustifiable use of force threatening security and integrity of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The submission thus advanced on legislative competence, more as a matter of form than with any feeling of conviction and belief in its merit, does not appear to be sound.
Precedent	447.
Precedent	<ORG> having been enacted under Entry 1 of List III of the Seventh Schedule, it did not suffer from lack of legislative competenc e, yet the question is if any of the provisions impinges upon the fundamental right guaranteed under the Constitution and is, therefore, ultra vires.
Precedent	Before embarking upon this exercise it may be worthwhile examining the depth of Article 21 of the Constitution as any law of punitive or preventive detention has to be tested on the touchstone of the constitutional assurance to every person that he shall not be deprived of his liberty except in accordance with procedure established by law.
Precedent	It is declaration of deep faith and belief in 3 1950 SCR 594: AIR 1950 SC 124: 51 Cri LJ 1514 19 (1966) 1 SCR 709: AIR 1966 SC 740: 1966 Cri LJ 608 human rights.
Precedent	In the "pattern of guarantee woven in Chapter III of the Constitution, personal liberty of a man is at the root of Article 21".
Precedent	Modem history of human rights is struggle for freedom and independence of the man.
Precedent	One may call the right guaranteed under Article 21 as, 'natural right' or 'basic human right' but a society, committed to secure to its citizens "justice socia l, economic and political; liberty of thought, equality of status and liberty to promote amongst themselves fraternity" the foundation on which edifice of the Constitution has been structured could not have done otherwise than to provide for the human dignity and freedom as has been done by Article 21 of the Constitution which reads as under:  "21.
Precedent	Protection of life and personal liberty.
Precedent	No person s hall be deprived of his life or personal liberty except according to procedure established by law."
Precedent	Each expression used in this article enhances human dignity and value.
Precedent	It lays foundation for a society where rule of law has primacy and not arbitrary or capricious exercise of power.
Precedent	'Life' dictionarily means, "state of functional activity and continual change peculiar to organised matter, and especially to the portion of it constituting an animal or plant before death; animate existence; being alive".
Precedent	But used in the Constitution it may not be mere existence.
Precedent	As far back as 1877 <NAME>, J. in <NAME> v. Illinois158  construed similar expression in the American Constitution as "more than animal existence".
Precedent	It has been approved by our Court in <CITATION_619152> .
Precedent	159 and reiterated in <NAME> v, <ORG> (1)23.
Precedent	It Was given new dimension in <CITATION_1766147> and extended in <CITATION_78536> when it was held: (SCR p. 529: SCC pp.
Precedent	618-619, para 8) "... protection of limb or faculty or does it go further and embrace something more.
Precedent	We think that the right to life includes the right to live with human dignity and all that goes along with it, namely, the bare necessaries of life such as adequate nutrition, clothing and shelter and facilities for reading, writing and expressing oneself in diverse forms, freely moving about and mixing and commingling with fellow human beings."
Precedent	448.
Precedent	Liberty is the most cherished possession of a man.
Precedent	"Truncate liberty in Article 21 and several other freedoms fade out automatically".
Precedent	<NAME> called it 'regulated freedom'.
Precedent	Liberty is the right of doing an act which the law per mits.
Precedent	This article instead of conferring the right, purposely uses negative expression.
Precedent	Obviously because the Constitution has recognised the existence of the right in every man.
Precedent	It was not to be guaranteed or created.
Precedent	One inherits it by birth.
Precedent	This absolutism has not been curtailed or eroded.
Precedent	Restriction has been placed on exercise of power by the <ORG> by 158 94 US 113 (1877) 159 (1964) 1 SCR 332: AIR 1963 SC 1295: (1963) 2 Cri LJ 329 23 : 1979 SCC (Cri) 155: (1979) 1 SCR 392 21 : (1978) 2 SCR 621 74 ; 1981 SCC (Cri) 212: (1981) 2 SCR 516 using the negative.
Precedent	It is <ORG> which is restrained from interfering with freedom of life and liberty except in accordance with the procedure established by law.
Precedent	Use of the word 'deprive' is of great significance.
Precedent	According to the dictionary it means, "debar from enjoyment; prevent (child etc.)
Precedent	from having normal home life".
RatioOfTheDecision	Since deprivation of right of any person by the <ORG> is prohibited except in accordance with procedure established by law, it is to be construed stri ctly against the <ORG> and in favour of the person whose rights are affected.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 21 is a constitutional command to <ORG> to preserve the basic human rights of every person.
RatioOfTheDecision	Existence of right and its preservation has, thus, to be construed liberally and expansively.
RatioOfTheDecision	As a corollary to it the exercise of power by the <ORG> has to be construed narrowly and restrictively.
RatioOfTheDecision	It should be so understood and interpreted as not to nullify the basic purpose of the guarantee.
RatioOfTheDecision	No legislative or executive action can be permitted to get through unless it passes through the judicial scanning of it being not violative of the cherished right preserved constitutionally.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the article is construed as empowering the <ORG> to make a law and deprive a person as the Constitution permits it then the entire concept of personal liberty shall stand frustrated.
RatioOfTheDecision	A political party voted to power may adopt repressive measures against its political foes by enacting a law and it may well be said that deprivation being in accordance with  procedure established by law it is within the constitutional frame.
RatioOfTheDecision	The procedure adopted by <ORG> either legislatively or executively must therefore satisfy the basic and fundamental requirement of being fair and just.
RatioOfTheDecision	The word 'except' restricts the right of the <ORG> by directing it not to fiddle with this guarantee, unless it enacts a law which must withstand the test of Article 13.
RatioOfTheDecision	Today it appears wellnigh settled that procedure established by law, extends both to the substantive and procedural law.
RatioOfTheDecision	Further mere law is not sufficient.
RatioOfTheDecision	It must be fair and just law.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even in absence of any provision as in American Constitution fair trial has been rendered the basic and primary test through which a legislative and executive action must pass.
Precedent	449.
Precedent	How fundamental is the guarantee under Article 21 of the Constitution can be well appreciated when one looks at the constitutional amendment made in the year 1978.
Precedent	By the 44th Amendment Act , 1978, Article 359 was amended and it was provided that Articles 20 and 2 1 could not be suspended even during emergency.
Precedent	The occasion for it arose due to narrow construction placed by this Court in <CITATION_165099707> Shuklal6O denying a citizen his right to challenge even arbitrary detention and arrest.
Precedent	450.
RatioOfTheDecision	Having analysed the scope of Article 21 and traced its history, judicially and legislatively, it is proposed to take up few provisions of 1987 Act as I have nothing to add to what has been said by Brother <NAME>, J., on 1984 Act and 1985 Act with which I respectfully agree.
RatioOfTheDecision	Taking up 1987 Act 1 may mention at the very outset that fully agree with the reasoning and conclusions arrived at by Brother <NAME>, J., in respect of most of the sections.
RatioOfTheDecision	For instance, I agree with him that sub-clause (i) of the definition of 160 : AIR 1976 SC 1207 'abet' should be amended in order to avoid the ambiguity and make it immune from arbitrariness.
RatioOfTheDecision	As regards Sections 3 and 4 they are not liable to be struck down for vagueness.
Precedent	Their scope has been elaborately discussed by Brother <NAME> , J.
Precedent	But the one section with which I could not reconcile, even though it was raised in written submissions, only, is Section 5 which is extracted below :  "5.
Precedent	Possession of certain unauthorised arms, etc.
Precedent	in specified areas.- Where any person is in possession of any arms and ammunition specified in Columns 2 and 3 of Category 1 or Category III(a) of Schedule 1 to the Arms Rules, 1962, or bombs, dynamite or other explosive substances unauthorisedly in a notified area, he shall, notwithstanding anything contained in any other law for the time being in force, be punishable with imprisonment for a term which shall not be less than five years but which may extend to imprisonment for life and shall also be liable to fine."
Precedent	451.
Precedent	Mere possession of arms and ammunition specified in the section has been made substantive offence.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is much serious in nature and graver in impact as it results in prosecution of a man irrespective of his association or connection with a terrorist or terrorist activity.
RatioOfTheDecision	A comparison of t his section with Sections 3 and 4 demonstrates the arbitrariness inherent in it.
RatioOfTheDecision	Section 3 operates when a person not only intends to overawe the Government or create terror in people etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	but he uses the arms and ammunitions which results in death or is likely to cause death and damage to property etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	In other words, a person becomes a terrorist or is guilty of terrorist activity when intention, action and consequence all the three ingredients are found to exist.
RatioOfTheDecision	Similarly Section 4 applies to those activities which are directed towards disrupting sovereignty and territorial integrity of the country.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus a terrorist or a disruptionist and a person possessing any of the arms and ammunition mentioned in the section have been placed on a par.
RatioOfTheDecision	In Sections 3 and 4 the offence arises on the act having been done whereas in Section 5 it is founded only on possession.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even under sub- section (3) of Section 3 a person is liable to be prosecuted for abetting the offence if he assists or communicates with a terrorist.
RatioOfTheDecision	Sub-sections (5) and (6) inserted by Act 43 of 1993 to Section 3 also require that a person can be prosecuted only if he is found to be a member of a terrorist gang or terrorist organisation etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Act , therefore, visualises prosecution of the terrorist or disruptionist for offenses under Sections 3 and 4 and of others only if they are associated or related with it.
RatioOfTheDecision	That is in keeping with the objective of the Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	The legislation has been upheld as the legislature is competent to enact in respect of a crime which is not otherwise covered by any Entry in List II of the Seventh Schedule.
RatioOfTheDecision	The definition of the crime, as has been discussed earlier, is contained in Sections 3 and 4 of the Act and it is true that while defining the crime it is open to the legislature to make provision which may serve the objective of the legislation and from a wider point of view one may say that possession of such arms, the use of which may lead to terrorist activity, should be taken as one of the offenses as a preventive or  deterrent provision.
RatioOfTheDecision	Yet there must be some inter-relation between the two, howsoever, remote it may be.
RatioOfTheDecision	The harshness of the provisions is apparent as all those provisions of the Act for prosecuting a person including forfeiture of property, denial of bail etc., are applicable to a person accused of possessing any arms and ammunition as one who is charged for an offence under Sections 3 and 4 of the Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is no doubt true that no one has justification to have such arms and ammunitions as are mentioned in Section 5 , but unjustifiable possession does not make a person a terrorist or disruptionist.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even under Ireland Emergency Provisions Act, 1978 on which great reliance was placed by learned Additional Solicitor General there is no such harsh provision like Section 5 .
RatioOfTheDecision	Since both the substantive and procedural law apply to a terrorist and disruptionist or a terrorist act or a disruptive act, it is necessary, in my opinion, that this section if it has to be immune from attack of arbitrariness, may be invoked  only if there is some material to show that the person who was possessed of the arms intended it to be used for terrorist or disruptionist activity or it was an arm and ammunition which in fact was used.
RatioOfTheDecision	452.I agree with Brother <NAME>, J., in respect of Sections 8 , 9 , 10 and II except that I would like to add that no one should be appointed as <ORG> who has retired from the service.
RatioOfTheDecision	I also agree with him on construction of other sections but coming to Section 15 the then Hon'ble Minister who piloted the Bill while advocating for conferring power on police officer to record the confession and for making it admissible supported the departure from age-old law in Evidence Act by taking illustration of England and America where confessions are permitted to be recorded by police constable.
RatioOfTheDecision	He made an appeal that a confession made to a police constable in those countries was admissible and, therefore, a time has come when this country should depart from what Sir <NAME> felt when Indian  Evidence Act , 1872 was enacted and a policeman was not treated as worthy of trust.
RatioOfTheDecision	In support of giving power to higher police officers to record confession he stated : (<ORG>ary Debates, p. 724) "Perhaps it is correct and good among many levels of <ORG> Officers.
RatioOfTheDecision	But are we going to live with that kind of a slur on the entire police for 120 years?
Fact	Are we going to say for ever and ever there will be nobody in the police force, no Indian, no son, no daughter in India, if he or she joins the police force will ever be fair just and objective?
Fact	All our children will join the police force.
Fact	They will rise to be the SP of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	They will rise to be DIG of <ORG> and IG of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Yet, is <ORG> going to say for ever and ever that this will be the only country in the world where a confession to a high police officer, whatever the safeguards, will be an untrustworthy statement?
RatioOfTheDecision	Are we going to live with that kind of a slur?
RatioOfTheDecision	What we are trying to do is, for a period of two years, in an extraordinary situa tion, dealing only with one kind of offence namely, terrorist offenses, we say, that a confession made to a high police officer of the rank of Superintendent of Police and above, under very restricted conditions, will be admissible in a court of law."
RatioOfTheDecision	The appeal made by the then Hon'ble Minister might appear plausible.
RatioOfTheDecision	There may be no difficulty in even sharing his views that at some point of time the distrust with which the police is looked upon has to be given up.
RatioOfTheDecision	But has the time come for that?
RatioOfTheDecision	Was the political, administrative and social climate of the country mature for it?
RatioOfTheDecision	What should not be forgotten is that it is not the efficiency or honesty of the police force at higher level which was relevant for taking such a momentous decision.
RatioOfTheDecision	What was required to be considered was if the approach of the police force has undergone a change.
RatioOfTheDecision	It would not be out of place to extract a paragraph from Fifth National Police Commission Report:  "41.30 We find that policemen have a tendency to become cynical.
RatioOfTheDecision	We also  find that frequently such cynicism is developed, within very few years of service.
RatioOfTheDecision	Policemen very rapidly pick up the knowledge that what the law requires is one thing but what has actually to be done in practice is another.
RatioOfTheDecision	Once this dichotomy takes root in their minds, all training, all exhortations are a waste.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus, the law is that third degree is not permitted, but in practice that is the only way.
RatioOfTheDecision	Very often people themselves expect the police to beat up goondas and when this is not done charges of bribery and corruption are hurled at the police.
Precedent	People complain that police are partial in their conduct, but policemen learn that while under the law all are equal, as things happen, a rich man is more equal than a poor man, a common citizen different from a politician or one who has the support of a politician, a bureaucrat different from an ordinary government employee the list is endless."
Precedent	453.
Precedent	When Evidence Act of 1872 was passed it was enacted by a <ORG> which was committed to rule the country a nd not govern.
Precedent	Yet the power to record confession was not entrusted to police officer.
Precedent	The rationale is not far to seek.
Precedent	There is a basic difference between the approach of a police officer and a judicial officer.
Precedent	A judicial officer is trained and tuned to reach the final goal by a fair procedure.
Precedent	The basis of a civilized jurisprudence is that the procedure by which a person is sent behind the bars should be fair, honest and just.
Precedent	A conviction obtained unfairly has never been countenanced by a system which is wedded to rule of law.
RatioOfTheDecision	A police officer is trained to achieve the result irrespective of the means and method which is employed to achieve it.
RatioOfTheDecision	So long the goal is achieved the means are irrelevant and this philosophy does not change by hierarchy of the officers.
RatioOfTheDecision	A Sub-Inspector of the Police may be uncouth in his approach and harsh in his behavior as compared to a Superintendent of Police or Additional Superintendent of Police or any higher officer.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the basic philosophy of the two remains the same.
RatioOfTheDecision	T he Inspector of Police is as much interested in achieving the result by securing confession of an accused person as the Superintendent of Police.
RatioOfTheDecision	By their training and approach they are different.
RatioOfTheDecision	Procedural fairness does not have much meaning for them.
RatioOfTheDecision	It may appear unfortunate that even after independence a force which was created to implement harsh and Draconian laws of imperial regime, ruthlessly and mercilessly, has not changed much even in people regime.
RatioOfTheDecision	Dignity of the individual and liberty of person the basic philosophy of Constitution has still not percolated and reached the bottom of the hierarchy as the constabulary is still not accountable to public and unlike British police it is highly centralised administrative instrumentality meant to wield its stick and spread awe by harsh voice more for the executive than for the law and society.
RatioOfTheDecision	One of the reasons for it may be, as observed by <ORG>, the political set-up of the country which has used it more to serve its purpose tha n to serve the society.
Precedent	454.
Precedent	The police constable in England and America is duty-bound to inform the accused not only that whatever he was going to state could be treated as confession in a court of law but he was entitled-to have his lawyer and any relative he desired.
Precedent	Section 62 of Criminal Law Act of 1973 of England made it mandatory for the police officer arresting a person to send information to his relative about arrest and place of detention.
Precedent	Circular No.
Precedent	74 of 1978 issued in England permitted the accused to have assistance of lawyer.
Precedent	In America same safeguards are provided by judicial decisions.
Precedent	In Section 24 of the Evidence Act a confession obtained by threat or inducement or by force is rendered inadmissible.
RatioOfTheDecision	By Section 25 a confession made to a police officer is deemed to be inadmissible ipso facto.
RatioOfTheDecision	But if the same confession is made in presence of a Magistrate then by Section 26 it is not treated as suspect.
RatioOfTheDecision	The obvious reason for these provisions is to ensure fair trial.
RatioOfTheDecision	A confession made to a p olice officer is suspect even in England and America.
RatioOfTheDecision	But it has been made admissible subject to the safeguards mentioned above.
RatioOfTheDecision	Why?
RatioOfTheDecision	Because what is provided by Section 26 of the Evidence Act stands substituted by presence of lawyer or near relative.
RatioOfTheDecision	A confession to a police officer in presence of a Magistrate is admissible as it having been recorded in presence of judicial authority it becomes credible.
RatioOfTheDecision	Same credibility attaches if the confession is recorded in England and America before a lawyer or near relative.
RatioOfTheDecision	Presence of Magistrate under Section 26 of the Evidence Act and of lawyer or relative in England and America lend credibility to the confession recorded by a police officer as the element of inducement, threat, duress or force stands removed.
RatioOfTheDecision	The inadmissibility attaches to a confession recorded by a police officer not because of him but because of uncertainty if the accused was not made a witness against himself by forcing out something which he would not have otherwise stated.
RatioOfTheDecision	Further a confessi on made to a police officer for an offence committed irrespective of its nature in non-notified area is inadmissible.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the same police officer is beyond reproach when it comes to notified area.
RatioOfTheDecision	An offence under TADA is considered to be more serious as compared to the one under Indian Penal Code or any other Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	Normally graver the offence more strict the procedural interpretation.
RatioOfTheDecision	But here it is just otherwise.
RatioOfTheDecision	What is inadmissible for a murder under Section 302 is admissible even against a person who abets or is possessed of the arms under Section 5 of the Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	How the methods applied by police in extracting confession has been deprecated by this <ORG> in series of decisions need not reproduced.
RatioOfTheDecision	But all that changed overnight when TADA was enacted.
RatioOfTheDecision	Giving power to police officer to record confession may be in line with what is being done in England and America.
RatioOfTheDecision	But that requires a change in outlook by the police.
RatioOfTheDecision	Before doing so the police force by education and training has to be made aware of their dut ies and responsibilities, as observed by <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The defect lies not in the personnel but in the culture.
RatioOfTheDecision	In a country where few are under law and there is no accountability, the cultural climate was not conducive for such a drastic change.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even when there was no Article 21 , Article 20(3) and Article 14 of the Constitution any confession to police officer was inadmissible.
Precedent	It has been the established procedure for more than a century and an essential part of criminal jurisprudence.
Precedent	It was, therefore, necessary to bring about change in outlook before making a provision the merits of which are attempted to be justified on law existing in other countries.
Precedent	455.
Precedent	Since for justifying various provisions of the Act reliance was placed on Ireland Emergency Provisions Act, 1978 and it was attempted to be argued that the provisions in the TADA were fair and just it appears necessary to say few words.
Precedent	In 1971 in England an internment operation of provisions was made which led to many arrests which were chall enged in <ORG>.
Precedent	And the <ORG> held that those exercising emergency powers were nonetheless required to fulfill ordinary common law requirement of informing the person arrested of the reason for his arrest.
Precedent	This led to constitution of <ORG> which resulted in Northern Ireland (Emergency Provisions) Act of 1973.
Precedent	This empowered the army to arrest any suspected terrorist for a period of four hours for establishing identity after which it was required to hand over the accused to the police.
Precedent	This led to abuse of power what came to be called, "military security" approach.
Precedent	And the survey made in that country noticed, "that the procedure for arrest and questioning and for extra judicial detention has been abused.
Precedent	The security authorities have in some areas mounted a 'dredging' operation based on widespread screening.
RatioOfTheDecision	This has resulted, in our view, in large numbers of wholly innocent persons being arrested and large numbers whose involvement in terrorist activities is relatively unimportant being  detained" (Terrorism and Criminal Justice by <NAME>).
RatioOfTheDecision	This led to replacement of 1973 Act by the new Act which is in force.
RatioOfTheDecision	Various safeguards were made in the Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	Sub-section (2) of Section 8 of the Act excludes any statement obtained by torture or inhuman treatment from admissibility.
RatioOfTheDecision	But Section 15 of the TADA throws all established norms only because it is recorded by a high police officer.
RatioOfTheDecision	In my opinion our social environment was not mature for such a drastic change as has been effected by Section 15 .
RatioOfTheDecision	It is destructive of basic values of the constitutional guarantee.
Precedent	456.
RatioOfTheDecision	A confession is an admission of guilt.
RatioOfTheDecision	The person making it states something against himself, therefore it should be made in surroundings which are free from suspicion.
RatioOfTheDecision	Otherwise it violates the constitutional guarantee under Article 20(3) that no person accused of an offence shall be compelled to be a witness against himself.
RatioOfTheDecision	The word 'offence' used in the article should be given its ordinary meaning.
RatioOfTheDecision	It applies as muc h to an offence committed under TADA as under any other Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	The word, 'compelled' ordinarily means 'by force'.
RatioOfTheDecision	This may take place positively and negatively.
RatioOfTheDecision	When one forces one to act in a manner desired by him it is compelling him to do that thing.
RatioOfTheDecision	Same may take place when one is prevented from doing a particular thing unless he agrees to do as desired.
RatioOfTheDecision	In either case it is compulsion.
RatioOfTheDecision	A confession made by an accused or obtained by him under coercion suffers from infirmity unless it is made freely and voluntarily.
RatioOfTheDecision	No civilised democratic country has accepted confession made by an accused before a police officer as voluntary and above suspicion, therefore, admissible in evidence.
RatioOfTheDecision	One of the established rule or norms accepted everywhere is that custodial confession is presumed to be tainted.
RatioOfTheDecision	The mere fact that the <ORG> was competent to make the law, as the offence under TADA is one which did not fall in any <ORG> entry, did not mean that the <ORG> was empowered to curtail or erode a person of hi s fundamental rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	Making a provision which has the effect of forcing a person to admit his guilt amounts to denial of the liberty.
RatioOfTheDecision	The class of offenses dealt by TADA may be different than other offenses but the offender under TADA is as much entitled to protection of Articles 20 and 21 as any other.
RatioOfTheDecision	The difference in nature of offence or the legislative competence to enact a law did not affect the fundamental rights guaranteed by Chapter III.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the construction as suggested by the learned Additional Solicitor General is accepted it shall result in taking the law back once again to the days of <NAME>, Section 15 cannot be held to be valid merely because it is as a result of law made by a body which has been found entitled to make the law.
RatioOfTheDecision	The law must still be fair and just as held by this <ORG>.
Precedent	A law which entitles a police officer to record confession and makes it admissible is thus violative of both Articles 20(3) and 21 of the Constitution.
Precedent	457.
RatioOfTheDecision	Section 19 provides for an appeal as a matter of  right from any judgment, sentence or order not being an interlocutory order of <ORG> to <ORG> both on facts and law.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such provision existed in 1984 and 1985 Act as well.
RatioOfTheDecision	It may be mentioned that when 1984 Act was passed by the <ORG>, it was primarily made due to grave situation prevailing in the State of Punjab.
RatioOfTheDecision	Today the 1987 Act has been extended even to far off States.
RatioOfTheDecision	The effect of such extension is that for every sentence, may be under Section 3 or 4 or any other section, one has to approach this Court.
RatioOfTheDecision	In many cases, the remedy of appeal may be illusory.
RatioOfTheDecision	For instance, one may be prosecuted under Sections 3 , 4 and 5 or under any other section and provision.
RatioOfTheDecision	He may be acquitted for the offenses under Sections 3 and 4 and yet may be convicted under other sections or provisions for minor offenses which were tried by <ORG> by virtue of Section 12 of the Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	He may not be able to approach this Court because of enormous expenditure and exorbitant legal expenses i nvolved in approaching this <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	It should not be forgotten that ours is a vast country with majority on the poorer side.
RatioOfTheDecision	The knowledge of economic inability of sizeable section of the society to approach this <ORG> by way of appeal may result in arbitrary exercise of power and excesses of the police.
RatioOfTheDecision	A provision for appeal to this <ORG> in minor cases may result in defeating the remedy itself.
Precedent	Inability to file appeal due to financial reasons in petty matters may amount to breach of 161 <CITATION_1857950> , AIR 1950 SC 27: 1950 SCR 88: 51 Cri LJ 1383 guarantee under Articles 14 and 21 of the Constitution.
Precedent	It may in many cases be denial of justice.
Precedent	I would, therefore, suggest that it may be examined if a proviso to sub-section (1) of Section 19 can be added that a person convicted of any offence other than Sections 3 and 4 of the Act shall be entitled to file an appeal in the High <ORG> under whose jurisdiction the Designated <ORG> is situated.
Precedent	Further in case the <ORG> files an appeal against acquittal of  the accused under Sections 3 and 4 in this Court then the appeal of the accused filed in <ORG> shall stand, automatically, transferred to this Court and shall be connected and heard along with appeal filed by the <ORG>.
Precedent	The <ORG> on such transfer should allow the accused to have a counsel of his choice the expenses for which should be home by the <ORG>.
Precedent	458.
Precedent	Coming to sub-section (8) of Section 20 one of the issues debated was if a person accused of an offence under the TADA was entitled to invoke extraordinary jurisdiction of <ORG> either for quashing of the proceedings as on facts no offence was made out and the proceedings were invoked as an abuse of process of court or for extraneous reason and whether the order rejecting the bail by <ORG> could be subjected to judicial review under Article 226.
Precedent	459.
Precedent	Law on the subject is fairly settled.
Precedent	<CITATION_1033637> a Bench of this Court of which one of us (Pandian, J.)
Argument	was a member, after detailed examination of the judicial dec isions held, "where the allegations made in the first information report or the complaint, even if they were taken at their face value and accepted in their entirety did not prima facie constitute any offence or make out a case against the accused", or " where the allegations made in the FIR or complaint are so absurd and inherently improbable on the basis of which no prudent person could ever reach a just conclusion that there was sufficient ground for proceeding against the accused", or "where a criminal proceeding was manifestly attended with <NAME> fides and/or where the proceeding is maliciously instituted with an ulterior motive for wreaking vengeance on the accused and with a view to spite him due to private and personal grudge", then the proceedings were liable to be quashed.
Precedent	In Usmanbhai53 it was conceded that a petition under Article 226 was maintainable.
Precedent	It was urged that the observation made by this <ORG> in Usmanbhai case53 in relation to exclusion of High <ORG>'s jurisdiction under Sections 439 an d 482 were squarely applicable to Article  226.
Precedent	Reliance was placed on <CITATION_499461> also.
Precedent	It was urged that as far back as <NAME> v. <NAME> it having been observed by this <ORG> that power of superintendence conferred by Article 227 was to be exercised most sparingly and only in appropriate cases in order to keep the subordinate courts within the bounds and their authority and not in correcting errors.
Precedent	The High <ORG> should not be permitted to entertain a petition against rejection of bail under 162 1992 Supp (1) SCC 335: 1992 SCC (Cri) 426 53  :1988 SCC (Cri) 318 123 : 1991 SCC (Cri) 265 118 1954SCR565:AIR1954SC215  Article 226 and 227.
Precedent	Reliance was also placed on <CITATION_376684> and <CITATION_26503> 21.
Precedent	The power given to High <ORG> under Article 226 is an extraordinary power not only to correct the manifest error but also to exercise it for sake of justice.
Precedent	Under the scheme of the Constitution a High <ORG> is the highest court for purposes of exercising civil, appellate, criminal or even c onstitutional jurisdiction so far that <ORG> is concerned.
Precedent	The jurisdiction possessed by it before coming into force of the Constitution was preserved by Article 225 and by Articles 226 and 227 an extraordinary jurisdiction was conferred on it to ensure that the subordinate authorities act not only in accordance with law but they also function within the framework of law.
Precedent	That jurisdiction of <ORG> has not been taken away and in fact could not be taken away by legislation.
Precedent	In England even in absence of Constitution whenever an attempt was made by <ORG> to provide that the order was final and no writ of certiorari would lie <ORG> always struck down the provision.
Precedent	Since <ORG> under the Constitution is a forum for enforcement of fundamental right of a citizen it cannot be denied the power to entertain a petition by a citizen claiming that the <ORG> machinery was abusing its power and was acting in violation of the constitutional guarantee.
Precedent	Rather it has a constitutional duty and r esponsibility to ensure that the State machinery was acting fairly and not on extraneous considerations.
RatioOfTheDecision	<CITATION_1577324> "' this Court after examining the principle laid down in <CITATION_1033637> and <CITATION_1256540> held that <ORG> has jurisdiction to entertain a petition under Article 226 in extreme cases.
RatioOfTheDecision	What are such extreme cases cannot be put in a strait- jacket.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the few on which there can be hardly any dispute are if <ORG> is of opinion that the proceedings under TADA were an abuse of process of court or taken for extraneous considerations or there was no material on record that a case under TADA was made out.
RatioOfTheDecision	If it be so then there is no reason why should <ORG> not exercise its jurisdiction and grant bail to the accused in those cases where one or the other exceptional ground is made out.

RatioOfTheDecision	Since I am agreeing with Brother <NAME>, J. except on one or two issues it appears appropriate to record my conclusions in brief:  (1) That the three Acts Act 61 of 1984,  Act 31 of 1985, and Act 28 of 1987 have been validly enacted by the <ORG> in exercise of its power under Entry 1 of List III of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	(2) Even though no opinion has been expressed by Brother <NAME>, J., on Section 5  1 am of the opinion that the provisions of this section can be invoked only when the prosecution is able to establish that there was 119 (1968) 3 SCR 692: AIR 1968 SC 1481 121  117  159 (1964) 1 SCR 332: AIR 1963 SC 1295: (1963) 2 Cri LJ 329 163 : 1993 SCC (Cri) 13 some material on record to show that the arms and ammunition mentioned in the section were likely to be used for any terrorist or disruptive activity or that they had been used as such.
RatioOfTheDecision	(3) Although the provisions relating to appointment of a person as <ORG> are clear yet in the written arguments it was pointed out that some of them were appointed even after retirement.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such appointments would be in teeth of the express provisions in the statute.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, no one should be appointed as <ORG> w <NAME> has retired from service.
RatioOfTheDecision	(4) As regards Section 15 of the Act which provides for recording of confession by Superintendent of Police, for the detailed reasons given by me I am of the opinion that it is violative of Articles 20 and 21 of the Constitution and, therefore, is liable to be struck down.
RatioOfTheDecision	(5) As regards provisions of appeal I have suggested that it may be examined by the appropriate aut<NAME>rity if a proviso could be added to Section 19 that where convictions are for offenses other than Sections 3 and 4 of Act 28 of 1987 the accused may be entitled to file an appeal in <ORG> itself and in case an appeal against conviction is filed by the Government in this Court then the appeal filed by the accused in <ORG> s<NAME>uld stand automatically transferred.
RatioOfTheDecision	I am further of the opinion that in such cases the accused s<NAME>uld be provided a counsel of his c<NAME>ice and the payment of fee s<NAME>uld be either made by the <ORG> or if made by the accused it s<NAME>uld be reimbursed.
RatioOfTheDecision	(6) As regards jurisdicti on of <ORG> to entertain an application for bail under Article 226 of the Constitution I am of the opinion that <ORG>s being constitutionally obliged to ensure that any authority which exercises judicial and quasi- judicial powers in its jurisdiction functions within the framework of law, are entitled to entertain the petition to determine if the proceedings were not an abuse of process of court.
RatioOfTheDecision	But while exercising discretion the court must not be oblivious of the sensitivity of the legislation and the social objective inherent in it and, therefore, should exercise it for the sake of justice in rare and exceptional cases the details of which cannot be fixed by any rigid formula.
